’ ‡§ß‡•Ä‡§∞‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ç ‡§∑‡•ã‡§°‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•Å‡§≠‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç- ’ ‡§ß‡•Ä‡§∞‡§æ‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•å ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§Ç ‡§π ’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§ó‡§§‡•ã ‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É ‡•§‡•§

VarunÃ£a
Vasi·π£·π≠ha MaitrƒÅvaru·πái
8 verses: tri·π£·π≠ubh
This hymn and the following three hymns (VII.87‚Äì89) are justly famous because they have an unusually intimate tone and because the poet Vasi·π£·π≠ha as a literary creation of the poem emerges as a distinct personality. Jamison (2007: 91‚Äì118) offers a detailed study of the hymns in this group and of the creation of the poet‚Äôs personality. Like the other hymns in this small collection, this poem centers on just two figures, the poet‚ÄîVasi·π£·π≠ha according to tradition‚Äîand the god Varu·πáa, and Vasi·π£·π≠ha speaks personally, even confessionally with Varu·πáa. Apparently Vasi·π£·π≠ha has been suffering from some kind of affliction, which, he has been told, is the pun ishment of King Varu·πáa for an offense. He begs Varu·πáa to accept his offerings and repentance, to forgive his transgressions, which he says were not intentional (vs. 6), and to restore him to prosperity and health.
The relationship between Vasi·π£·π≠ha and Varu·πáa constantly shifts as the hymn unfolds. The literary strategy of the poem most clearly appears in its use of pro nouns and other markers of person, as Jamison (2007: 96‚Äì100) shows in her analysis. Use of the 3rd person distances the speaker and the object, while addressing another as ‚Äúyou‚Äù is a token of closeness and presence to one another. The poet deploys these pronouns first to distance Vasi·π£·π≠ha and Varu·πáa, then to bring them close, and finally to separate them once again. So in verse 1 Vasi·π£·π≠ha is absent because there is no reference to the speaker, and Varu·πáa is distanced by the 3rd singular pronoun asya. His absence is mitigated only by fact that unaccented asya should be anaphoric and therefore might point to the previous presence of the god. Otherwise, the relation
ship between Vasi·π£·π≠ha and Varu·πáa is completely broken. In verse 2 Vasi·π£·π≠ha is present (1st sg. in each of the four lines), and Varu·πáa remains distanced. In verse 3 Vasi·π£·π≠ha is present once again (1st sg.) and Varu·πáa is partly present: he is addressed in the vocative but he is also mentioned in the 3rd person. In verse 4 both Vasi·π£·π≠ha (1st sg.) and Varu·πáa (2nd sg.) are present. Then there begins a retreat from their full presence to one another. In verse 5 Vasi·π£·π≠ha is less present (the 1st pl. generalizes rather than personalizes the speaker) and Varu·πáa is present (through the vocative address and 2nd sg. imperative). This verse reverses 3, in which Vasi·π£·π≠ha was fully present but Varu·πáa partly present. In verse 6 Vasi·π£·π≠ha is distanced (note especially svaÃÅ ‚Äúone‚Äôs own,‚Äù not ‚Äúmy own‚Äù), and Varu·πáa is present (through the vocative address). Again this verse reverses 2, in which Vasi·π£·π≠ha was present and Varu·πáa was distanced. In verse 7 Vasi·π£·π≠ha is present (1st sg.), but Varu·πáa is distanced (3rd sg.). This partially reverses 1 insofar as Vasi·π£·π≠ha was absent there but is present here. Varu·πáa remains distanced in both verses 1 and 7. The last verse (8), is a concluding, extra-hymnic verse, a coda that stands outside the main structure of the hymn.
This analysis also shows the omphalos structure of the hymn. Verse 1 corresponds to 7, verse 2 to 6, verse 3 to 5, and verse 4 is the center. It is the thematic heart of the hymn because it is in this verse that the poet and the god are both present and pres ent to one another. It also signifies the determination of Vasi·π£·π≠ha, who anticipates the resolution of his conflict with Varu·πáa. Varu·πáa will‚Äîperhaps even in the sense of ‚Äúmust‚Äù‚Äîproclaim what Vasi·π£·π≠ha has done, and Vasi·π£·π≠ha promises to make amends to the god to receive the god‚Äôs forgiveness. Although the hymn moves away from this encounter between Vasi·π£·π≠ha and Varu·πáa, the last verse suggests that the problem has been resolved. Varu·πáa has returned to the distance, but he and Vasi·π£·π≠ha now mutually support one another. Varu·πáa has enlightened Vasi·π£·π≠ha (vs. 7c), and Vasi·π£·π≠ha ritually serves the god to his greater ‚Äúwealth,‚Äù his power and glory (7d).
In verse 2b the meaning of vaÃÅru·πáe ‚Äúwithin Varu·πáa‚Äù may play on the possible ety mological relation of vaÃÅru·πáa and vrataÃÅ ‚Äúcommandment.‚Äù That is, ‚Äúwithin Varu·πáa‚Äù has the sense of ‚Äúunder, or in conformity with, the commandment of Varu·πáa.‚Äù The poet wants to be no longer at odds with Varu·πáa. At the same time the hope for closeness to the god or even for envelopment by the god, which is suggested in the phrase, looks forward to the increasing intimacy of Vasi·π£·π≠ha and Varu·πáa. The state is both parallel to and contrasts with the situation in verse 6c where the elder is ‚Äúwithin the misdeed of the younger.‚Äù
In verse 4d, we read √°va…ƒ´yƒÅm, from ava + ‚àöyƒÅ ‚Äúmake recompense,‚Äù instead of the transmitted but metrically faulty √°va…iyƒÅm.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúPermanent in greatness are the births of that Varu·πáa who propped up the vast heaven and earth, whoappointed to (their) two-fold (task) the glorious sun and beautiful constellations, who spread out the earth.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Theirtwo-fold task: dvitƒÅ: of giving light by day and by night

Jamison Brereton

Insightful are the races (of gods and mortals) through the greatness of him who propped apart the two wide world-halves.
He pushed forth the vault of heaven to be high and lofty, (also) the star [=the sun] once again, and he spread out the earth.

Griffith

WISE, verily, are creatures through his greatness who stayed ever, spacious heaven and earth asunder;
Who urged the high and mighty sky to motion, the Star of old, and spread the earth before him.

Erst durch seine Gr√∂√üe sind die Gesch√∂pfe weise geworden, der die beiden Welten, obwohl sie so weit sind, auseinander gestemmt hat. Er hat hoch nach dem Himmel gesto√üen, doppelt dem Himmelsgestirn einen Ansto√ü gegeben, und er hat die Erde ausgebreitet.

Grassmann

Voll Macht und Weisheit ist doch dessen Wesen, der Erd’ und Himmel festigte, die weiten, Die hehre Himmelsw√∂lbung hoch emportrieb, das Sternenheer, der Erde Fluren aufthat.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúWhen may I in my person n converse with the deity? When may I (be admitted) to the heart of Varu·πáa?By what means may he, without displeasure, accept my oblation? When may I, rejoicing in mind, behold thatgiver of felicity?‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

And together with my own self, I speak this: ‚ÄúWhen shall I be within Varu·πáa?
Might he take pleasure in my offering, becoming free of anger? When shall I, with good thoughts, look upon his mercy?‚Äù

Griffith

With mine own heart I commune on the question how Varuna and I may be united.
What gift of mine will he accept unangered? When may I calmly look and find him gracious?

Geldner

Und ich gehe mit mir selbst dar√ºber zu Rate: Wann werde ich wohl dem Varuna nahe kommen? Wird er frei von Groll sich meines Opfers freuen? Wann werde ich wohlgemut seine Gnade schauen?

Grassmann

Und mit mir selber sprech’ ich diese Worte: ¬ªWann werd’ ich doch mit Varuna vereint sein? Ob er mein Opfer ohne Z√ºrnen annimmt? wann werd’ ich seine Huld beseligt schauen?¬´

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúDesirous of beholding you, Varu·πáa, I inquire what is my offence; I have gone to make inquiry of thewise; the sages verily have said the same thing to me: – this Varu·πáa is displeased with you.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

I ask myself about this guilt, o Varu·πáa, wanting to see; I approach those who understand in order to inquire.
Even the sage poets say the very same thing to me: ‚ÄúVaru·πáa now is angry with you.‚Äù

Griffith

Fain to know this in I question others: I seek the wise, O Varuna, and ask them.
This one same answer even the sages gave me, “Surely this Varuna is angry with thee.”

(‡§§‡§§‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§π (‡§è‡§®‡§É) ‡§™‡§æ‡§™ (‡§™‡•É‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á) ‡§Ü‡§™ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§™‡•Ç‡§õ‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•Ç‡§Å (‡§â‡§™‡•ã, ‡§¶‡§ø‡§¶‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å) ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡•á ‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§® ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∑‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡•à‡§Ç (‡§ö‡§ø‡§ï‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§∑‡§É) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§•‡§æ ‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§ï‡§∞ (‡§è‡§Æ‡§ø) ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§ä‡§Å, (‡§ï‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ (‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡•É‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§Ç) ‡§≠‡§≤‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§™‡•Ç‡§õ‡§®‡•á ‡§™‡§∞ (‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç) ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç (‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ù‡§ï‡•ã (‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï (‡§Ü‡§π‡•Å‡§É) ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§ï‡§π‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, (‡§π) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø (‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç) ‡§Ø‡§π (‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§É) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ (‡§§‡•Å‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç) ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§ï‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§á‡§§‡•ç) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ï‡•á (‡§π‡•É‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡•á) ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§â‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§ì‡§∞ ‡§≤‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§æ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à‡••‡•©‡••

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ï ! ‡§Æ‡•à‡§Ç ‡§â‡§® ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ï‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡•Ç‡§Å, ‡§ú‡§ø‡§®‡§ï‡•á ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡•á ‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§® ‡§∏‡•á ‡§µ‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡§π‡•Ç‡§Å‡•§‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§ï ! ‡§ê‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•É‡§™‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§Æ‡•à‡§Ç ‡§â‡§® ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§õ‡•Ç‡§ü‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§â‡§Å‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ ‡§™‡•Ç‡§õ‡§®‡•á ‡§™‡§∞ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§ï‡§π‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§≤, ‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§ø ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§ï ‡§Ö‡§Ç‡§∂‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§â‡§®‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§ì‡§∞ ‡§ù‡•Å‡§ï‡•á, ‡§§‡•ã ‡§µ‡§π ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§≤‡•Å ‡§≠‡§ó‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§â‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§á‡§∏‡§≤‡§ø‡§è ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§è ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§µ‡§π ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§π‡•ã‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã, ‡§§‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§â‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•ã ‡§∏‡§ï‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡••‡•©‡••

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§É) ‡§≠‡•ã‡§É ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§≠‡§ú‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç ! (‡§§‡§§‡•ç) ‡§§‡§§‡•ç (‡§è‡§®‡§É) ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§Ç (‡§™‡•É‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á)‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§™‡•É‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø (‡§â‡§™‡•ã, ‡§¶‡§ø‡§¶‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å) ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ø‡§¶‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§∞‡§π‡§Ç (‡§ö‡§ø‡§ï‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§∑‡§É) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§•‡§æ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡§É (‡§è‡§Æ‡§ø) ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç (‡§ï‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§É (‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡•É‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§™‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§É (‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§≠‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡•á (‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ç (‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§Ç (‡§Ü‡§π‡•Å‡§É) ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§Ç (‡§π) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Æ‡§ø‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç (‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§É) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡§à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§É (‡§§‡•Å‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§ï‡§Ç (‡§á‡§§‡•ç) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡•á‡§® (‡§π‡•É‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡•á) ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡•á‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§â‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø ‡§®‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡••‡•©‡••

‡§π‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£ ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§Ü‡§ó‡§É ‡§Ü‡§∏ ‡§ï‡•ã‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡•ã‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•ã‡§¨‡§≠‡•Ç‡§µ‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ø‡•á‡§® ‡§Ü‡§ó‡§∏‡§æ ‡§∏‡§ñ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§ø‡§ò‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§∏‡§ø ‡§π‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§∏‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§π‡•á ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§≥‡§≠ ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§¶‡§≠ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§¨‡§æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§æ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á‡§ú‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§π‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£ ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§ó‡§É ‡§Æ‡•á ‡§Æ‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•ã‡§ö‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ç‡§π‡§ø ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§É ‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§É ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§π‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§É ‡§∂‡•Ä‡§ò‡•ç‡§∞‡§É ‡§®‡§Æ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§®‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§µ‡•á‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡•™ ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúWhat has that great wickedness been, Varu·πáa, that you should seek to destroy the worshiper, yourfriend? Insuperable, resplendent Varu·πáa, declare it to me, so that, freed from sin, I may quick approach you withveneration.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Was the offense so very great, Varu·πáa, that you wish to smash a praise singer and companion?
You will declare this to me, o you hard to deceive, o you of independent will! With reverence I would swiftly *make recompense to you (to be) freed of guilt.

Griffith

What, Varuna, hath been my chief transgression, that thou wouldst slay the friend who sings thy praises?
Tell me, Unconquerable Lord, and quickly sinless will I approach thee with mine homage.

Geldner

Was war das gr√∂√üte Vergehen, Varuna, da√ü du deinen Freund, den S√§nger t√∂ten willst? Sage mir das an, du Untr√ºglicher, Eigenm√§chtiger! Ich m√∂chte dem zuvorkommend von S√ºnde befreit unter Verbeugung dir Abbitte tun.

Grassmann

Was, Varuna, war meine schwerste S√ºnde, dass du den S√§nger, der dich liebt, willst schlagen? Das k√ºnde mir, untr√ºglicher Beherrscher; durch diese Andacht m√∂cht’ ich dich bes√§nft’gen.

Elizarenkova

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£) ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç ! ‡§§‡§§‡•ç (‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç (‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•ç (‡§Ü‡§ó‡§É) ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§Æ‡•ç=‡§Ö‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§É (‡§Ü‡§∏) ‡§¨‡§≠‡•Ç‡§µ ‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§æ (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡•á‡§® ‡§π‡•á‡§§‡•Å‡§®‡§æ (‡§∏‡§ñ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§Ç (‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡•ã‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§ï‡§Ç (‡§ú‡§ø‡§ò‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§∏‡§ø) ‡§π‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§∏‡§ø (‡§§‡§§‡•ç) ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§Ç (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡•á‡§£ (‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø (‡§µ‡•ã‡§ö‡§É) ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É (‡§¶‡•Ç‡§≥‡§≠) ‡§π‡•á ‡§ú‡•á‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø ! Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúRelax (the bonds) imosed by all the ill deeds of our fore-fathers, and those incurred (by the sins) whichwe have committed in our person ns; liberate, royal Varu·πáa, like a calf from its tether, Vasi·π£·π≠ha like a thiefnourishing the animal (he has stolen).‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Like a calf: there are two comparisons: liberate Vasi·π£·π≠ha (i.e., me) like asa thief (is liberated), who (having performed his penance for the theft he has committed, at its termination) feedsthe animals (with food), and (i.e., or) like a calf from its tether: pa≈õut·πõpam na tƒÅyum stainyaprƒÅya≈õcittamk·πõtvƒÅvasƒÅne ghƒÅsƒÅdibhi·∏• pa≈õ≈´nƒÅm tarpayitƒÅram stena iva damno rajjo·∏• vatsam na vatsam iva cavasi·π£·π≠ham mƒÅm bandhakƒÅt pƒÅpƒÅt avas·πõja vimu√±ca

Jamison Brereton

Release from us ancestral deceits and those that we ourselves have committed.
O king, release Vasi·π£·π≠ha from his bond like a cattle-stealing thief, like a calf.

Griffith

Free us from sins committed by our fathers, from those wherein we have ourselves offended.
O King, loose, like a thief who feeds the cattle, as from the cord a calf, set free Vasistha.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúIt is not our own choice, Varu·πáa, not our condition, (that is the cause of our sinning); it is that which isintoxication, wrath, gambling, ignorance; there is a senior in the proximity of the junior; even a dream is aprovocation to sin.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Our condition: na sa svo dak·π£o dhruti·∏• = the condition appointed by destiny at the time ofbirth is the cause of the committal of sin, not our own power or will, dhruti·∏• stirotpattisamaye nirmitƒÅ daivagatihpƒÅpaprav·πõttau kƒÅra·πáam na svabh≈´tam balam;

Not our own will betrayed us, but seduction, thoughtlessness, Varuna wine, dice, or anger.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£) ‡§≠‡•ã‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡§≠‡§ó‡§µ‡§®‡•ç ! (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§É) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ (‡§¶‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§É) ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡§ø‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§§‡•á (‡§∏‡§É) ‡§§‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§Ç (‡§®) ‡§® ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§ø, ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á (‡§ß‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∏‡•Å ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§¶‡•É‡§¢‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§É (‡§∏‡§æ) ‡§∏‡•à‡§µ (‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•á‡§§‡•ã‡§É (‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É) ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§ß ‡§è‡§µ ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•å ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡•Ä‡§¶‡§ï‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡§™‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç, ‡§§‡§•‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ö (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á (‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç, ‡§ï‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§∏‡§É, ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•á) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ω‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø, ‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•Ñ‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§π‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•Ñ‡§®‡•ç ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§ö, (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡§É, ‡§ö‡§®, ‡§á‡§§‡•ç) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ (‡§Ö‡§®‡•É‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø, ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§æ) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•É‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§ø ‡••‡•¨‡••

‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§π‡•ç‡§≥‡•Å‡§∑‡•á ‡§∏‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§ï‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ú‡§ó‡§§‡•ã‡§≠‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§®‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§É ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§É ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§π‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ö‡§∞‡§£‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§¶‡§æ‡§∏‡•ã‡§® ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§≠‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ö‡§∞‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§ö ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§§‡•ã‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§ö‡•á‡§§- ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§™‡§Ø‡§§‡•Å ‡§ó‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§û‡•ç‡§ö ‡§ï‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§§‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§É ‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ß‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ß‡§®‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§ú‡•Å‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡§ø ‡§ú‡•Å‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•Å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§Ø‡§§‡•Å ‡•• ‡•≠ ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúLiberated from sin, I may perform diligent service, like a slave, to the divine showerer (of benefits), thesustainer of the world; may he, the divine lord, give intelligence to us who are devoid of understanding; may hewho is most wise, guide the worshipper to wealth.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Like a servant, I will give satisfaction to the generous (master); freed from offense, I (will give satisfaction) to the ardent one.
The civilizing god [=Varu·πáa] made those without understanding to
understand; the better sage poet [=Vasi·π£·π≠ha] speeds his clever (patron) [=Varu·πáa] to riches.

Griffith

Slavelike may I do service to the Bounteous, serve, free from sin, the God inclined to anger.
This gentle Lord gives wisdom to the simple: the wiser God leads on the wise to riches.

Geldner

Ich will dir dienstbar sein wie ein Knecht dem Lohnherrn, ich dem ungeduldigen Gotte, von der S√ºndenschuld befreit. Gott der Herr unterwies die Unwissenden. Den Geschickten spornt der noch weisere Gott zum Reichtum an.

Grassmann

Dem Knechte gleich will ich dem gn√§d’gen dienen, Von Schuld befreit dem eifervollen Gotte; Die Thoren, die ihm treu, hat er belehret, den Klugen f√ºhrt der Weisere zum Heile.

Elizarenkova

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ö‡§π‡§Ç) ‡§Æ‡•à‡§Ç (‡§Ö‡§®‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§É) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§™ ‡§π‡•ã‡§ï‡§∞ (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§∏‡•á (‡§¶‡§æ‡§∏‡§É, ‡§®) ‡§¶‡§æ‡§∏ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§Ç, ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø) ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§æ‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§è ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•Ç‡§Å, (‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§≥‡•ç‡§π‡•Å‡§∑‡•á) ‡§µ‡§π ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§´‡§≤‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§æ‡§§‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡§É, ‡§Ö‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó ‡§¨‡§§‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Ä (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§É) ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§ï‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§∞‡§É) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ (‡§ó‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§Ç) ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡•á, ‡§ú‡•Å‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡§ø) ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§ì‡§∞ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§ø‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡••‡•≠‡••

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§™‡§•‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§ï ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§ï‡§•‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§π‡•á ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ! ‡§Æ‡•à‡§Ç ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§§ ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•Ä ‡§π‡•Ç‡§Å ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§ï‡•É‡§™‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü‡§™ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§∞‡•á ‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ù‡•á ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡••‡•≠‡••

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø (‡§∏‡§Ç) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§æ‡§µ‡§É ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£ ‡§§‡•Å‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§É ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§è‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ï‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§Æ‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§¶‡•É‡§¶‡§ø ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•á ‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡•Å ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡•Å ‡§â‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡§â‡§™‡§ó‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§µ‡•á- ‡§§‡•á‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§ø‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§É ‡§Ö‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡§£‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§Æ‡§É ‡§®‡•ã‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§Æ‡•á ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡•á ‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∂‡§Æ‡§®‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•á‡§ö ‡§®‡•ã‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡§£‡•á ‡§∂‡§Æ‡•Å ‡§∂‡§Æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§π‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡•ã‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§É ‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§®‡•ã‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§∂‡•à‡§É ‡§™‡§æ‡§§ ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§ ‡•• ‡•Æ ‡••‡§∞‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§•‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•Å‡§≠‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§û‡•ç‡§ö-‡§∞‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§ó‡§§‡•ã‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É ‡•§

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúMay this laudation, food-conferring Varu·πáa, be taken to your heart; may success be ours in retainingwhat we have, and in acquiring more; and do you, (deities), ever cherish us with blessings.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

This praise song is for you, Varu·πáa, you who are of independent will: let it be set within your heart.
Let there be good fortune in peaceful settlement for us and let there
be good fortune in war for us. ‚Äì Do you protect us always with your blessings.

Griffith

O Lord, O Varuna, may this laudation come close to thed and lie within thy spirit.
May it be well with us in rest and labour. Preserve us ever-more, ye Gods, with blessings.

Geldner

Dieses Loblied soll dir fein, du eigenm√§chtiger Varuna, recht ans Herz gelegt sein. Gl√ºck werde uns im Frieden, Gl√ºck auf der Kriegsfahrt! Beh√ºtet ihr uns immerdar mit eurem Segen!

Grassmann

Es m√∂ge dies mein Loblied, o gewalt’ger, o Varuna, so recht ans Herz dir dringen; Heil sei beim Ruhn uns, Heil uns bei der Arbeit. Ihr G√∂tter, sch√ºtzt uns stets mit eurem Segen.

Elizarenkova

(‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡§ø) ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§Æ‡•á‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§™‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§Ü‡§™ (‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§è (‡§∂‡§Ç) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§π‡•ã‡§Ç (‡§ä‡§Å) ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•á, ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó=‡§Ö‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§Æ=‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§ú‡§ø‡§è, ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§∏‡•á (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§≤‡§Æ‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á (‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§∏‡§¶‡§æ) ‡§∏‡§¶‡§æ (‡§™‡§æ‡§§) ‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç‡••‡•Æ‡••

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§ó‡§à ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§π‡•á ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç ! ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§π‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã, ‡§Ü‡§™ ‡§ï‡•É‡§™‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ï‡•á ‡§π‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó-‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§π‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§ß‡§ø‡§ï ‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§¶‡•à‡§µ ‡§∞‡§§ ‡§∞‡§π‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§â‡§®‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§Æ-‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§π ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§è ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§â‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡••‡•Æ‡•• ‡§Ø‡§π ‡•Æ‡•¨‡§µ‡§æ‡§Å ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡•Æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Å ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡••
